Company.
DAMAGE SUIT IN PROSPECT
Theatrical Managers Recount Their
Troubles With the Pert Manipu
lators of the Switches.
In the advertisement of the Tivoll
Opera-house that appears this week is
the following brief declaration of war
against the Pacific Telephone and Tele
graph Company:
On account of unsatisfac
tory telephone service no or- .
ders for seats will be received
hereafter by telephone.
This announcement is the culmination
of a long list of complaints by patrons of
the house, concluding with a vigorous
protest by John I. Sabin, the president of
the telephone company, to Manager
Leahy, and his receiving a practical il
lustration of the unreliability of his ser
vice. Incidentally it may be mentioned
Mr. Sabin's protest will probably result
in a suit for damages against his com
pany.
Mr. Leahy explained the trouble to a
Call reporter last evening as follows:
Fur years we have kept a telephone in the
box ofiice for the convenience of our patrons,
but the telephone is gone now, and hereatter
anybody wishing to secure seats in advance
will have to cu.il for them In person or send
somebody. At first the innovation worked sat
isfactorily and was a great convenience ,iu peo
ple, but uf late it has only been a source of
annoyance and the cause df frequent complaint;;
against the employes of the house.
Frequently people have come to the box of
fice and demanded why they were unable to
call up by telephone, saying they had tried
repeatedly and each time had been told that
the line was busy. Then again orders have
been received for seats in a certain portion
of the house, as the man in the box office un
derstood the order, but when the parties ar
rived they declared they had called tor entirely
different seats and would refuse to be con
vinced that the misunderstanding was due to
the telephone working poorly. Everybody who
has to use a telephone frequently knows how
oMen he is switcned on to another line from
the one he wants and his mesepge goes astray,
while he appreciates the difficulty in getting
"Central," and having succeeded in getting tlie
haughty young woman there to respond to his
calls the average man knows he is utterly un
able to induce her to Rive him the number he
wants until it suits her own sweet fancy to
do so.
Our trouble with the telephone service reach
ed a climax one night last week, when Mr.
Sabin, the president of the company, called
me out of my office and proceeded to denounce
the employes for what he termed, among other
thinÃ^, their discourtesy and incivility. It ap
pears that <lurlng the day Mr. Sabin called up
the box-office by telephone and asked to have
some seats reserved for him. The treasurer
replied that it would be done and Sabin asked
what row the seats were In. The reply was
that there was nothing left better man the
eighteenth row, which Sabin said would an
swer. When he came in B mi found where his
seats were he raised a great fuss, declaring j
the man in the box-office had told him they
were in the eighth row, and when told that he j
must have misunderstood he claimed he had I
been treated discourteously.
I explained to Mr. Sabin that his was only
one case in many where patrons had found the j
telephone service unsatisfactory and that I
had concluded to put a stop to further com- i
plaints by havine the instrument removed.
The telephone we had in here was one of {
those profanity-promoting devices known as
the nickel-in-the-slot telephone, from which j
the renter receives a certain percentage of the
receipts after they have reached a certain
amount. Our contract called for the telephone
up to the first of the month, but early the
following morning agents of the company re
moved the telephone, taking with them all the
cash in the box, a portion of which belonged
to us. For this palpable violation of Its con
tract we shall bring suit for damages agÃlnst
the company, and I have already placed the
matter in competent lawyers' hands. The
service was wretched, but my contract called
for the ÃBe of the telephone to April 1, and I
Interd to make Mr. Sabin pay for his act.
When he made his complaint of discourteous |
treatment by my employes, I told him he i
would <lo well to Instruct his nwn employes be- |
fore flndine fault with ethers. A more un
civil set than the telephone operators it would
be hard to find anywhere, in my judgment.
Manager Fnealander of the Columbia.
Baldwin and California theaters said he
had received many complaints from pa
trons of his houses about the poor tele
phone service, but he had not thought
about discontinuing the practice of re
ceiving: orders for seats in that manner.
He had found it did not always work to
the satisfaction of those who tried it; but
as long as the public found it a conveni
ence and was will.ng to take chances on
the poor service he was willing to accom
modate it. -!;-_-.
Manager MorriÃsey of theOrpheum was
outspoken in his denunciation of the t> 1 â
phone service. "We keep one man in the
box office most of the time." said he,
"whose duty it is to answer the tele
phone, and a ward job he has of it. The
trouble is, the service is operated on t<io
iheap a scale. Ihe girls are underpaid
and overworked, and, consequently, are
careless in answering calls and making
switches. The greatest fault, however. Is
the lack of facilities. Too many tele- 1
phones are maintained on each circuit,
and this causes great confusion and de
lay, besides preventing anything like
privacy. Almost every time one uses a
telephone he overhears conversations
between others, while he waits in vain
for the party he wants to answer his call. |
"The greatest complaint we have to
make Is the maddening persistency with
which 'central' rings our bell when she
wants us. Frequently the bell rings a
full minute after one of us removes the
receiver from the hook, and all efforts to
induce the operator to stop the din are
unavailing.
"I receive numerous letters from pa
trons complaining that they are unable to
.secure Peats by telephone, but it Is not
our fault if the telephone company will
not connect usÂ»."
The theatrical managers are not the
only people to complain of the miserable
telephone service. It has been growing
worse steadily, until it has reached a
stage where many subscribers are follow
ing the course of the Tivoli and are dis
pensing with the service altogether.
In the Divorce Courts.
Eva H. Oyster was granted a divorce
from her husband, George M. Oyster, yes
terday on the ground of failure to pro
vide. Ella R. Clark was granted a di
vorce from W. J. Clark on the ground of
failure to provide. Emma Pauline Bryant
was granted a divorce from George Bry
ant yesterday on the ground of willful
desertion. The plaintiff was allowed to
resume her maiden name. Wilbur.

For a Writ of Mandate.
Kntherine M. Ball applied yesterday to
the Superior Court to compel the Board of
Education to restore her to her position
as teacher of special drawing, from which
position she was removed in May, 1897.
A temporary writ was granted by the
presiding judge and made returnable in
Department No. 7 on March 28.
TO DRILL ON
THEIR OWN LOT
Tower Being Erected for
the Use of the Fire
Laddies.
Chief Sullivan Desirous of Es
tablishing a Pompier
Corps.
They Have Done Splendid Service in
Eastern Cities in Saving
Lives at Fires.
The necessity for having a conven
ient place for fire drill purposes has
long been apparent to Chief Sullivan,
as the present mode has had many
drawbacks. It has been the custom to
select some building every Sunday
morning, as the extramen could not at
tend any other day, but the constant
complaints from residents in the neigh
borhood was a source of annoyance to
the Chief and the men.
To put a Btop to all these annoy
ances it was decided recently to erect*a
tower on a lot in the rear of engine
house No. 32, on Seventeenth street,
near Folsom, and it will be finished
How the Tower Will Lock When
Finished.
about the end of this month. It will
be 75 feet high with a slope at the bot
tom to give It strength. It will have
a metallic stand and lire escape, such
as are used on many of the building*
In the city, and the men will be drilled
In that kind of work, connecting the
ladders with the stand pipe so as to
get the hose to a certain position or to
tho roof if necessary.
It is the ambition of the Chief to start
a pompier corns in connection with the
department, but unfortunately this is
impracticable until the department, or
at least a portion, is placed under the
fully paid system wltn the men con
tinually on duty. With this object
partly in view of the Chief in his last
annual report recommended the follow
ing: "I respectfully recommend that
all the companies of this department
now responding to alarms of fire in
the mercantile district of the city be
placed under a fully paid system, with
I the entire force continually on duty, in
accordance with and as authorized by
the provisions of an act of the Legisla
ture, approved March 4, ISO 7, entitled
'An act to provide for increasing the ef
ficiency of fire departments within mu
nicipalities of the first class in the State
of California.' "
A pompier corps was first introduced
in St. Louis about twenty years ago
and since then corps have been estab
lished in New York, Boston and all
large Eastern cities, and they have
done splendid service in saving lives
and property, especially in New York.
An attempt was made about fifteen
years ago to establish a corps here, but
it had to be given up, as it was im
possible to get the men properly drilled
under the "call" system. The men
must be constantly on duty to make an
efficient pompier corps.
The pompier ladder is a hickory stick
partly in vie wthe Chief in his last
about fourteen feet high and on each
side are handles for placing the feet
upon. Each team has three pompiers
and each man has a belt around his
waist with a snap which he attaches
to the pompier, enabling him to use
both his hands in hoisting the pompier
to the next story window. By thai
means they can climb all over a build
ing.

In Memory of their Saint and Martyrs
A GRAND patriotic celebration of St.
Patrick's day will be held this after
noon and evening: at Woodward's
Pavilion. It will be under the aus
pices of the Irish societies of San
Francisco. The entire proceeds will be
donated to the '98 Centennial and Monu
ment Association.
The celebration this year is of more
than ordinary significance, for It marks
the centennial of the spirited rebellion of
Tnited Ireland in JTfIS and the seml-cen
tenlal of the Young Ireland movement of
1848. The memory of the patriots who
sacrificed their lives in 1798 and IMS in a
vain endoavor to free their country from
oppression Is still fresh in the minds of all
'irishmen and the lovers of liberty. St.
? Patrick, the patron s^aint of the little isle
'of green, taught the Irish people the love
of liberty. To his teachings is due the
\ patriotism that .swells the breasts of
Irishmen all over the world. They have
always been found In the ranks of those
opposed to tyranny and despotism, fight
ing for the grajid and noble principle that
all men are born free and equal.
The proceeds of the entertainments at
Woodward's Pavilion will be remitted to
the committee in Dublin having charge
of the fund for the erection of a monu
ment to Theobald Wolfe Tone and the
other patriots who gave up their lives In
17;Â»S in the noble struggle for the freedom,
of Ireland. The general admission to
each entertainment will be 25 cents, with
reserved seats 25 cents extra. The after
noon exercises will commence promptly
.at 2 o'clock, when the following pro
gramme will be curried out:
Overture, Irish A!rs..Urennan's Military Band
Remarks, President of the day,. Frank Conklln

"Walter Ranker Pullman, aged 23
years, a native of Illinois, secured a
license In Oakland last night to wed
Miss Louisa Lander West, aged 24
years, a native of California. The wed
ding was billed to take place shortly
after the license was issued and the
party came to San Francisco.

Mrs. Hugh McDonnell, younger, but
not more com^y than her sister,
Louisa, was married some seven or
eight months ago to a mining man,
Hugh McDonnell. The wedding took
place in New York- and the conven
tional bridal tour to Europe followed.
The wedding journey lasted three
months, and then McDonnell and his
bride came to California, taking apart
ments on the sixth floor of the Palace
Hotel. For a time the bride's mother
dwelt in the beautiful suite and joined
her married daughter and her beauti
ful Louisa in lovely excursions. The
McDonald equipage was the finest in
the court of the Palace, and when the
stately and stunning ladies were es
corted to the carriage the spectators
gazed in admiration at so much Cali
fornia beauty of the Amazonian type.
Louisa loved the Palace Hotel, and It
was charming to dwell there with her
sister, who had married a rich mining
man, who was abundantly able to be
stow all the comforts and nearly all
the luxuries of life in this latitude.
Hugh McDonnell, the husband, went
En st. and the elegant Louisa put the
name of the Palace Hotel on her new
copper plate, hence her engraved visit
ing cards announce her days at the
Palace. Mr. McDonnell's great wealth
was acquired in mining. The Iron
Mountain mine of Shasta County pro
duced a large share of his income. At
last accounts he was in New York
looking after his mining interests, but
his family still abides at the Palace.
The West young ladies, estimable and
attractive, were not widely known in

much inquiry last evening about the

WEST POINTERS
MEET AT DINNER
Anniversary of the Founding
of the Academy Is Duly
Commemorated.
Happy Gathering of Graduates Now
on This Coast in Honor of
ths Day.
The anniversary of the founding of
West Point Military Academy was cele
brated by the graduates of that institu
tion on this coast by a banquet at the
Malson Rfche last evening. This was the
second annual dinner held here in com
memoration of the anniversary and It is
the intention of the graduates to make it
a yearly event. Forty-eight g-uests at
tended the dinner, over which the veteran
Colonel George Mendell presided. At the i
conclusion of tliu repast he Introduced j
Captain Pettit as toastmaster. Captain !
Payson of the Valley Railroad responded
to the toast, "Graduates in Civil Life,"
after which Professor Soule of the Uni
versity of California spoke on "The Army
and the Union." To Lieutenant Wilson,
Third Artillery, fell the pleasing duty of
responding to- "Wives and Sweethearts."
These were the only set speeches of the
evening, but everybody present was called
upon for some informal remarks and it
was midnight when the Last glass was
drained and the company broke up. The '
following gentlemen were in attendance: j
Colonel J. Stewart, Professor Frank Soule,
Lieutenant J. S. Oyster. GeiK'ral <). D. Greene,

Rival Doctors Quarrel.
Myron Mills, known in business circles
as Dr. Allen, was arrested yesterday for
practicing medicine without a license.
James Smith, a Japanese, was the com
plaining witness. Mills was taken to the
California street station and hooked. Af
t< r paining his release on bail, he opened
up his lecture hall at Pine and Kearny
sttreets.and filled it comfortably with the
enticing strains of a flageolet. 'Dr. Allen
says his arrest was instigated by a busi
ness rival, who keens a similar estab
lishment in the same neighborhood, and
who was jealous of his success.

HELD UP ON A
PUBLIC STREET
Alex Anderson Obliged
to Give His Valuables
to Masked Men.
First Asked the Time of Day
and Then Thoroughly
Searched.
Started to Hun but Changed His
Mind and Reported the Matter
to the Police.
Masked men nrp again In ovldenco.
lyrist evening 1 at about 9 o'clock Alex An
derson, while on his way home, was
stopped at Mission and Ecker streets by
two men, who wore masks and carried re
volvers In their hands. He was first asked
the time of day, but seeing the disguise
which the men wore he started to run
down Mission street, but was promptly
told to stop and throw up his hands.
He did as was bid, and was soon re
lieved of $8 40. He was then told to move
on and not to look around or there would
be trouble. Ag^in he complied with the
request, and, according to the statement
given to thÂ»- police by the victim, he was
glad to escape with his life. The neigh
borhood where the hold up took place Is
rather dark at that time in the evening,
and it is probable the men took advan
tage of the fact.
From the report mnde to police head
quarters Anderson was walking along not
thinklns of danger when he was stopped
in a. sudden manner by a heavy set man
and asked what time it was. He started
to pull out his watch when he saw the
Bhining end of a revolver, and immr-di
ately took in the situation and started
on a run down the street. A command
to halt stopped him, mid besides losing
the money which he carried, he was re
lieved of his watch. After the robbers
were through with him he was allowed
to go, but with an order that he was to
keep silent or he would regret it. Silence
according to orders was maintained, but
the victim proceeded to police headquar
ters where he reported the matter to the
Chief of Police, and detectives were de
tailed on the case.
From a description given by the robbed
man the highwaymen differed altogether
as one was stout and short while the
other was slim and tall. The police are
of the opinion that they know who the
men are. and it is probable that arrests
will follow to-day.

ONLY A FAKE.
W. H. Crocker's Health Is Not Im-
paired â He Is Traveling Because
He Likes That Kind of Life.
The story that W. H. Crocker's health
is failing and that he is under the care
of a specialist at Carlsbad Is nothing
more or less than a fnke. Mr. and Mrs.
Crocker left San Francisco about three
months ago for a leisurely journey
through Europe. They sojourned at Nice
for several weeks and wrote to their
friends concerning the pleasures of their
trip. While Mr. Crocker is a wealthy
man, holding vast interests in California,
ami the president of one of the leading
banks of the city, his affairs are in such
shape by reason of the organization of
the Crocker Estate Company, that he is
not tied down to his office. Ills large in
terests in the Southern Pacilic Railroad
arc managed by the Crocker Estate Com
pany, but he has not personal inclina
tion to take an active part in the man

Ancient Order of Hibernians, Division
No. 2, will give a ball this evening at
B'nai B'rith Hall.
Delmonico's banquet hall has been in
the hands of the decorators for some
days past. The adornments of this spa
cious room, it is intended, will be the
finest in its history. The red. white ani
blue will be blended with Krin's "im
mortal green" in silken devices, and the
florists of the city have furnished their
choicest plants and will send in great
hampers of flowers so as to make it
beautiful. Additional electric lights have
been added to give brilliance to the scene.
The occasion for which all this prepara
tion has been made is St. Patrick's day,
but St. Patrick's day with the glory of
the '9S centennial added. The Knights of
St. Patrick determined some time ago
that this year there would be special ob
servances In honor of the martyrs and
heroes of '9S at their annual banquet and
they made their plans accordingly. It Is
expected fully 250 guests will be present.
The opening address will be by Mr. Shea,
and then in order will come the following
toasts: "The Day We Celebrate."' Hon.
James D. Phelan; "United States of
America." Hon. W. W. Foote: "Civil and
Religious Liberty," James R. Kelly; "Our
Kindred Societies." John C. Reid of the
Caledonian Club; "Ninety-Eight," John J.
Barrett.
IN HONOR OF ST. PATRICK'S DAY
Interesting Exercises Given by the
La Salle Literary Club in Sacred
Heart College.
Lnst evening the large assembly hall
In Sacred Heart College was filled to the
limit to hear the exercises given in honor
of St. Patrick's day by the De la Salle
Literary and Debating Club. The hall
was decorated with green and white
streamers, which intermingled at vari
ous points with the red, white and blue.
The programme was interesting and at
tentively listened to by all present. All
the members who took part in the pro
gramme were heartily cheered for their
efforts.

TRADE COMING BACK.
Parties for Alaska Arrive From Dis
tant Places to Outfit Here.
Since the Alaska information work has
been assumed by the State Board of
Trade and the exhibit moved from tha
Pavilion back to the depot building at
the foot of Market street, times around
the headquarters are livening up consid
erably. Manager Filcher expresses him
self as pleased with the work his Alaska
Information Bureau is performing for
San Francisco, and with the outlook for
better results to follow future efforts.
His correspondence and the incoming peoÂ«
pie impress him that, notwithstanding
the cut rates to northern ports, there is
still a great interest in San Francisco by
those contemplating a trip to the north
ern gold fields. The advantages which
this port offers by reason of its nearnesa
to the base of the principal Alaskan sup
plies and of its larger trade facilities
seem to be realized by many, and among
such the disposition is to take advantage
of them. Mr. Nolan, in charge of tno
outside offTco, claims that there are more
bona-iide inquirers than there has been
since the office was opened. There are not
so many local curiosity seekers around
the place, nor so many cappers for fake
outfitters. This latter class have been
given emphatically to understand that
their prcscnop fit the rooms of the bu
reau's headquarters would net be tol
erated.
Monday there was a party of twelve
from Indian Territory, who were here
on their way to Alaska, and among the
visitors yesterday were two gentlemen
from Bntte, Mont., who had come this
way because they believed they could get
their supplies enough cheaper arid better
in San Francisco to offset the difference
in the passenger rate by the northern
route.
Manager Filcher is convincod that with
proper backing and proper effort ener
getically and judiciously displayed San
Francisco can reap its full share of what
ever benefit may arise from the travel to
Alaska. He says if the business com
munity will stand by the State Board of
Trade in this work he is convinced that
the results in the end will fully justify
thfir confidence and support. As soon as
travel opens by way of the Yukon River
he thinks this city, by proper offort, can
secure the butt end of the travel.

Koe Estate Loses a Fortune.
Judge Slack yesterday rendered judg
ment against the estate of George H.
Roe for $71,324 24 in favor of the Cali
fornia Electric Light Company. Roe was
a director in the plaintiff company at the
time of the sale of its property for $1,000,
000. A commission of $100,000 was paid on
the sale and it subsequently developed
that Roe, although a director in the com
pany, received $r.0.000, one-half of the
commission. The company held that Roe's
transaction was Illegal and suit was com
menced and yesterday Judge Slack found
for the plaintiff and allowed interest from
the date of the sale.

One of the most notorious opium dens

visited by a Call reporter and an art

the drug which De Quineey described as
<>ne that steals away one's life is ex

â visited. There were from two to ten

of opium. There were hardened users
of the drug, as well as young men and
women just falling into the vortex that
leads to physical and mental downfall.
The victims of the drug, in various
Ftages of intoxication from its sicken-
RIDICULOUS LAWS DEFEAT THE POLICE.
Sergeant Martin states the whole case of the helplessness of the po
lice in the following terse interview:
"We may go to the rooms and smell the smoke, we may even
see the victims of the Chinese habit puffing away at the pipes and
sinking into the dope fiend's dream, yet we are powerless to make
them quit. Why? Because the law says that the offense consists
not in smoking opium, but buying and smoking it in the same place.
So if we do not see the victim buy the opium and smoke the same
particle which he bought it is impossible to secure a conviction. We
have even hired men to go to places and buy the drug, and we have
then lOBt the case because there was a doubt in the minds of those
trying it whether the opium we saw the defendant smoking was the
same 'shell' or load of the drug which he bought. The law is so tech
nically absurd that it could not have been worse for the police if it
had been drafted by the proprietors of the opium joints themselves.
"Thus, f what you say you saw is true. We know of many places
where the 'drug is sold and smoked, where it is kept on hand, and
where young men and wwmen are daily and nightly lured into the vile
habit, but what good does it do to make an arrest? We are de
feated almost every time we go into court and we weary of trying to
do anything in the way of enforcing the law. It is idleness and
child's play to be dragging people into court if we are laughed out
by reason of the weakness of our case. I see very little chance out
of this unless there should be an amendment of the very lame law
governing the subject."

Emergencyâ The guard has been called out
during the year for emergency as a test of Its
I ability sto respond. Results were, ; in most
i case.-i, quite- satisfactory. A report of an
i alarming character came from settlers .of
i Shasta County some months since as to â â  a
I threatened outbreak of Indians in that locality.
By order r the Governor of the State . I pro
ceeded to the scene of supposed j trouble j with
direction to make Investigations and take such
steps in the use of troops as 1 saw tit. In July
the Sheriff of Colusa County called for troops
to protect a - prisoner against mob violence.
; Two companies were placed at his disposal and
responded ' and performed their duty in a very
satisfactory manner. The Sheriff places great
value upon the support they gave him.
Equipment â cnange since last report, ex
cept that all troops have been equipped with
Webb cartridge belts. -
Horses â Horses are generally hired from liv
ery stables, and effort is made to procure the
same horses for each drill. â  These horses could,
no doubt, be purchased in on emergency, but
while they are c"~r< enough for the few drills
and parades â  the National Guard cavalry "have,
are generally unfit for . field service. Good
horses .are abundant in California, and I be
lieve a large supply could oe oecured in a very
short time entirely suited for . cavalry pur
poses in war. â  â¢-.â¢â â 
- Ammunition â The small ammunition, caliber
I 45, is purchased from San Francisco firms. It
is of good quality and very similar to that
manufactured by the United States Govern
ment. For target practice each regiment re
loads.
Each company keeps on hand in its armory
1000 rounds.
â  Camp and garrison equipage company
has in its armory from ten to thirteen wall
tents, mostly of private manufacture and in
good serviceable condition. These tents are
somewhat larger than the army wall tent and
! furnish ample covering for the men. A few
1 companies have the army wall tent. The sup
! ply of . army hospital tents is ample for the
present force. No shelter tents are owned by
the State! or in the hands of the troops. All
the companies are now provided with field
mess outfits and Duzzacott ovens.
Property accountabilityâ Captains of com
panies are required to give bonds for $2000 and
to render a quarterly return of property to the
Adjutant-General of the State. No property
Is dropped from returns except by transfer,
condemnation, etc. Regulations are now
strictly enforced.
. Subsistenceâ Rations â The subsistence de
partment is Inoperative except when troops are
In camp or called Into active service. Jn these
cases rations are supplied through the senior
commissary officer, present generally through
contract, though emergency purchases are made
from time to time in open market. The army
ration has been adopted and prescribed, though
but little adhered to up to the present time.
In recent encampments the first attempt was
made to issue rations regularly, and, except
In one regiment, each company messed itself.
The commissary officers all found that there
were complaints of insufficiency, but, as I
predicted, this dissatisfaction greatly de
creased. The companies, being now provided
with Buzzacott ovens and individual mess out
fits, prepared their own meals, their first cook
being generally a hired man, assisted by an
enlisted man.
There was some dissatisfaction with the Bus
zacott oven, which I believe, from my expe
rience with the oven, results largely from the
lack of knowledge of its use.
â¢ Drills and Ceremonies â Companies are re
quired to -aye three drills a month, except In
December. Battalion drills have been held In
all the regiments. The First Infantry, sta
tioned entirely in San Francisco, has had great
advantage in this particular over the other
regiments, none of which has more than two
or three companies located together, though
the encampment brought them all . together,
and very considerable progress was made in
battalion and regimental drills. All companies
have made great improvement In close-order
drill, and with the exception of two or three
are entirely proficient, many drilling quite as
well as companies of regular troops. In ex
tended order there has likewise been great Im
provement during the year, and a greater num
ber of companies have attained proficiency.
The encampments held during the year have
given the opportunity to begin the work of
field exercises. Written reports were required
from commanding officers and umpires of all
problems, which reports, were thoroughly dis
cussed and commented- upon before all the
officers. The Intelligent manner In which the
field maneuvers were executed and subsequent
ly reported and discussed was highly satisfac
tory and showed that great interest was taken
by officers and men.
As a result of the Interest awakened some
of the regimental commanders are now arrang
ing to assemble their commands 'â¢ for bivouac
over Saturday night for an exercise in minor
tactics on the following Sunday. I have
offered my services for all such occasions and
will endeavor to make them both Instructive
and interesting.
Guard Duty â recommend to regimental com
manders the practice of guard duty while in
barracks, so that upon going Into camp their
men would have some knowledge of their duty
on euard. The camps are of such duration
that it is Impossible to make good sentinels of
green men, when they do only one tour of
guard duty during the encampment. Only a
limited amount of outpost duty was done in
connection with the field exercises in camps.
Target Practiceâ my last report the
scope of target practice has been extended.
This practice is held at home stations. Dur
ing the encampment considerable practice was
held especially In the- Seventh Infantry- In
addition to this, all the infantry companies
held . a competitive firing, silhouette targets,
this scheme of firing having been arranged by
the inspector-general of rifle practice, and con
sisted of firing at the G target at known dis
tances, twenty shots being fired between COO
and 200 yards. . .
Armoriesâ The State owns no armories, but
rents buildings for the quartering of the guard.
The buildings occupied by the San Francisco
regimerts were constructed for armory pur
poses but are only fairly suitable. Neither
armory is large enough for the entire regi
ment, eight companies of which occupy one
and four the other. | The matter of State own
ership of its armories in large cities is being
Recommendationsâ necessity of replacing
the old and worn Springfield rifles now in the
hands of the guard with serviceable rifles of
the model of 18S4 is urgent. The resolution for
exchange which passed Congress last session i
was so worded as to apply to but a few States. !
This exchange should be made at as early a
date as possible or the efficiency of the troops
will be proa! ly reduced in case an emergency
should arise for their employment.
THE BUCCANEER.
Hearst Must Pay Several Thousand
Dollars for Carrying Fake Dis
patches Without a License.
Vessels are not to be licensed as yachts
while employed for business purposes.
This decision was given in the matter of
W. R. Heart's yacht Buccaneer, em
ployed in conveying fake dispatches be
tween Havana and Key West. The Solic
itor of the Treasury, to whom the matter
had been referred, expressed the opinion
that the Buccaneer was evidently not
employed exclusively as a pleasure ves
sel, and was, therefore, not entitled to
the privilege of a vessel licensed as a
yacht under the laws of the United
States. â
The Secretary of the Treasury invites
the attention of the Collector of Customs
at Key West to the penalty of $200 fine
imposed for each trip of such vessel, in
addition to any other penalty that might
be imposed by law. -; .'

"I think Ripans Tabules about the best tning
there is," said the Colonel; "I guess they are THE
best ! I was sitting the other evening smoking and drink-
ing a little whisky and water ; there were four of us there
and one complained of being very uncomfortable. When
he had told us about it I said to him, * all that ails you is
indigestion,' and I gave him a Ripans Tabule from a little
vial I had in my pocket We each of us took one, and
since then I have learned that every one of three has
bought the TABULES and thinks them the greatest
things there are a going."

Silver a fraction higher-
Wheat dull and easy.
Barley rules firm.

Feeds-tuffs unchanged.

More Eastern Poultry in.

More damage to Fruit by frost.
Dried Fruit flat.
Provisions still dull.
Hides Irregular.
Meat market unchanged,

EXPLANATION.
The arrow flies with the wind. The top fig
ures at station indlcu.e maximum temperature
for the days, those underneath It, If any. the
amount of rainfall, of melted snow In Inches
and hundredths during the past twelve hours.
Isobars, or solid lines, connect points of equal
air pressure; Isotherms, or dotted lines, equal
temperature. The word "high" means high
barometric pressure and is usually accompanied
by fair weather; "low" refers to low pres
sure and is usually preceded and accompanied
by cloudy weather and rains. "Lows" usually
first appear on the Washington coast. When
the pressure is high in the interior and low
Blong the coast, and the Isobars extend north
and south along the coast, rain is probable;
but when the "low" Is inclosed with isobars of
marked curvature, rain south of Oregon is Im
probable. With a "hlph" In the vicinity of
Idaho, and the pressure falling to the Cali
fornia coast, wanner weather may be expected
In summer and colder weather In winter. The
reverse of these conditions will produoe aa
opposite result.

FORECASTS.
The pressure has rispn rapidly over the upper
Missouri valley and slowly over the greater
IÂ»Tt:i>n of the Pacific coast. During the past
twelve hours there has been a Flight fall along
the coast of Washington. An area of low
pressure lies over Southeastern California and
Arizona.
The temperature has remained nearly sta
. tlonary over the entire rour'.ry west of the
Rocky Mountains. Throughout California the
temperature Is from 5 to 10 deg. below the
â¢ normal.
Showers are reported throughout Central
California. A trace of snow fell at San Fran
cisco.
Heavy and killing frosts occurred generally
throughout California this morning. Condi
tions are favorable for (Yosts again Thursday
morning, though probably less severe.

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK. March 16.â The early strength
In to-day's stock market was a repetition of
yesterday's movements, but prices resisted the
late pressure for a decline, and as a conse
quence show substantial net gains all through
the list. The bulls had the advantage also
of higher prices on the London Exchange, and
although prices Immediately rose buoyantly
above the London parity there was conspicuous
buying for London as late as business was
done In the British capital. The London buy-
Ing was certainly due to a feeling at that cen
ter that there is a more promising prospect
aa to affairs between the United States and
Spain. This feeling was shown also In a quite
buoyant tone for the Spanish 4s, which rose
1% in London and % in Paris. There was un
doubtedly prevalent in New York also a feel
ing that the outlook was more peaceful, and
such developments as had a threatening aspect
were Ignored. Either the Spanish Government s
semi-official announcement of its opinion on
the maintenance by the United States of the
fleet in Florida waters or the news that official
attention had been called to this opinion by the
Spanish Government would have been sufficient
to demoralize the market a week ago. But
the underlying conviction in Wall street is that
the authorities at Washington have come to
Indulge a hope of a peaceful settlement. The
continued preparations for defense and such
incidents aa those of to-day do not wholly dis
sipate this conviction. Nevertheless, operations
are now on a large scale pending a definite
decision of the Cuban problem. The supply
of stocks was not large to-day, and prices
moved easily upward without any heavy trans
actions. A part of the buying was attributed
to large bull interests, but much of It was also
supposed to be the covering of the short sell-
Ing which caused the late decline yesterday.
The bears were apparently averse to renewing
their attempt to-day, and the high level of
prices was not seriously impaired. The hopes
of a successful adjustment of the traffic rate
controversy In the Northwest was quite an im
portant factor In the advance here and in Lon
don for Americans. The local and Industrial
specialties led to-day's advance, but the
grangers, Northwesterns and international
stocks In the railway list were also conspicu
ously strong. Heavy additional engagements
of gold for Import were made to-day without
affecting the steadiness of the exchange mar
ket below the gold import point. The total
gold importation from Europe on this move
ment has now reached $24,000,000, and it shows
no sign of subsiding In spite of the growing
tightness of. money In London and in European
centers.
The accumulated stocks of exchange are sup
posed to have been somewhat reduced, but
there Is undoubtedly a large supply still re
maining, and the continued heavy outward
movement of merchandise Is constantly bring
ing new bills Into the market which are sold
against gold imports as fast as they come
forward.
Bonds were strong throughout in sympathy
with stocks. Total sales, $1,830,000.
United States new 4s, the 5s and the old 4s
coupon each advanced % bid to-day.
Total sales of stocks to-day, 372.900 shares,
Including: Atehison preferred, 5020; Burling
ton, 42.900; L. and N.. 9855: Manhattan, 27,135;
Metropolitan, 100,330: Missouri Pacific, 4645;
New York Central, 4320: Northern Pacific, 7320;
do preferred. 27,305; Rock Island. 13.950; St.
Paul, 4680; U. P.. 4320; Tobacco. 32.434T People's
Gas, 5605; Sugar, 34.616; Western Union, 3220.

LONDON MARKET.
NEW YORK, March 16.â The Evening Post's
i London financial cablegram says: There was
! a general improvement In the stock markets
here to-day, mainly due to the bears scramb
ling in. There was little' fresh buying. The
feature was the sharp rise In Spanish fours,
Americans and Canadian stocks, Americans
V>eing benefltedd by the impression that Spain
and the United States would do their utmost
to settle matters on diplomatic lines. The
market was favorably affected by hopeful
views of a speedy termination of the Canadian
Pacific rate war. The close was a trifle under
: the best, except for Canadians and Grand
Trunks, which were booming In the street at
top prices. Mines Joined in the general rally
and all foreigners and Soutn American stocks
were better, being led by a rise of 2 in Span
ish fours on the official announcement that the
April coupon will be paid.
Gold is still much wanted for New York,
but it Is believed In good quarters that If
politics Improve the demand for gold may ease.
The distinctly easier tone In to-day's market
here Is due to the announcement that on April
4 the Government will re-pay upward of $3,
000,000 treasuy bills.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
! CHICAGO. March 16.â Wheat was very weak
;at the opening, the bear crop sentiment
j among traders on last night's curb, resulting
j from the continuance of remarkably favorable
! weather prevailing over the entire wheat sec
; tion, which reflected fully. Opening prices for
j July ranged from 85% cto 85% c, against yester
: day's closing of 857ic. For a moment or two
' the market hesit cd. The opening decline in
I itself was sufficient to induce covering in a
< moderate degree and the market recovered a
, fraction, selling at 85% c. -mat, however, was
1 the extent of the bullishness. Offerings soon
' began to come in from all directions, holders
' apparently being discouraged at the excellent
I crop prospects. The decline also brought in
; stop-loss orders and the pressure was greatly
I increased by prominent St. Louis operators, of
j ferlngs from that source being exceptionally
I heavy. The market fell pretty steadily to 84c,
becoming quite nervous at around the bottom.
A reaction then ensued. Professional shorts
and many early sellers came In to cover lines
showing good profits, and by noon prices had
recovered to 85c. May was steadily held by the
Leiter interests at $1 04 throughout the ses
sion, although one or two sales were made at
$1 03%. Liverpool cbles showed weakness all
day. After noon the market again declined.
Shorts had by that time been filled up, and
when the market lost this supporting Influence
prices sagged at once. July was pounded down
to 84% c and was bringing 84%@84%c at the
close.
Fine weather and the wheat weakness were
the factors in corn. May closed %c lower.
Liquidation in oats was quite as free and
general. Wheat and cr>m were the influences.
May closed %c lower.
Provisions were in the main steady, consid
ering the weakness In -grain. At the close
May pork was 2%c lower. May lard 2%cÂ» tower
and May ribs unchanged.

BOSTON WOOL MARKET.
BOSTON, March 16. â The American Wool and
Cotton Reporter will say to-morrow of the wool
market: There has been scarcely any demand
for wool during the past week, the total sales
reported being even less than those of the pre
vious week.
Manufacturers are not in need of wool and
they manifest hut little Inclination to add to
their holdings until they know more definitely
how the goods market â '" shape itself. As a
rule prices are well maintained.
The sales of the week amount to 827,000
pounds domestic and 280.000 pounds foreign,
making a total of 1.107,000 pounds, against a
total of 1.237,000 pounds for the previous week
and a total of 7,161,000 for the corresponding
week last year. Sales since January 1, 1898,
amounted to 37.126.450 pounds, against 69,
775,700 pounds last year at this time.

THE STOCK MARKET.
On thÂ» Bond Exchange the sugar stocks
continued to harden and Oceanic Steamship
also advanced again.
The feature of mining stocks was the ad
vance in Sierra Ne%ada to $1 20 under favor
able reports from the mine. The east com
partment of the upraise above the Riley tun
nel is up 25 feet, with the top in $41 ore. The
west compartment is now being raised and is
up 30 feet; the top shows broken material
showing spots of ore.
Gould & Curry is assessed 15c and Utah sc.
At the annual meeting of the Chollar Min
ing Company the old board of directors was
re-elected for 1898, with Charles Hirshfield as
president, Charles E. Elliott secretary and
H. M. Gorham superintendent.
The Belvedere Land Company of Marin
County has levied an assessment of $1 per
share, delinquent April 20.
In the Comstock ground of the Savage Min
ing Company, on the 130 foot level, the main
west drift has been repaired and retimbered
a distance oÂ£' IS feet; total length 423 feet. On
the Brunswick lode incline shaft No. 1 has
been sunk 16 teet since last report; total depth
on the slope 112* feet. The main north drilt
on the 600 foot level hae been advanced IS feet;
total length, 335 feet from the south line; the
face is in very hard ground, composed of por
phyry and seams of low grade quartz.
In pursuance of the resolution recently
adopted by the San Franciseu Stock and Ex
change Board, amending its By-laws so as to
create auxiliary membership the following
printed form of application has been sent out
to business men in this city:
"Application for privilege of auxiliary mem
bership in the San Francisco Stock and Bond
â¢Exchange â The privilege consists as follows:
On favorable consideration by the Exchange,
the applicant, on payment of $100 per annum,
in advance, is entitled to be on the floor of
the Exchange during all its business hours,
and to all the rights and privileges of regular
membersxof said Exchange. That Is. only in
so far as trading and dealing in the so-called
bonds, local and industrial securities are con
cerned. And be it further understood that
any one holding said privilege or auxiliary
membership is at all times subject to the
rules and regulations of said Exchange. Agree
ing to abide by all the conditions and terms
referred to above, I hereby make application."
In the Crown Point mine the Joint Belcher
and Crown Point drift from the top of the
Joint upraise from the 850 foot level is out 20
feet, 10 feet having been run during the week;
the face is in quartz giving low assays.
In the Belcher mine, on the 1200-foot level,
the east crosscut from the north drift Is out
33 feet, 5 feet having b' â made during the
past week; the face is in quartz having no
value. There have been hoisted during the
week and stored In the ore house at the mine
42 mining carloads of ore, the average assay
value of which was $21 31 per ton.
The Segregated Belcher mine yielded 15 min
ing carloads of ore during the past week. The
average assay of samples taken from the cars
was $24 85 per ton. The ore is being stored in
the ore house at the mine.
The yield of the Overman mine for the past
week amounted to 4 carloads of ore from the
workings in the north drift on the 90rt-foot
level. The assay value of the ore, per car
samples, was $34 45 per ton. Much dead work
was done in the mine during the week.
The official letter from the Justice mine for
the past week says: "During the week our
force has been principally engaged in timber
ing the southeast drift from west crosscut No.
2. We have completed the station for a winze
at the face of the southeast drift, and com
menced sinking the winze to-day. In cutting
out the winze station we saved 4 tons of or*
this week assaying $45 33 per ton."

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United State* HydrographlO
Office, located In the Merchants' Exchange, la
maintained in Sun Francisco for the benefit of,
mariners without regard to nationality and
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially invited to visit the
office, where complete sets of charts and sail-
Ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison â¢ nd reference, and the latest
Information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers m navigation and all matter*
of interest to ocoan commerce.
The time ball m top of the nuildtng on Tele
graph Hill is hooted about ten minutes befor*
noon and is dropped at noon. 120 th meridian,
by telegraphic signal received each day from
the United States Naval Observatory at Marft
Island, Cal.
A notice statiiig whether the time ball was
dropped on time : or giving the error. If any, la
published the same day by the afternoon
papers, and by the mornine papers the follow
ing day. W. S. HUGHES,
Lieutenant. U. S. N., in charge.

POLITICIANS
NOT UNITED
Pardee Men Anxious to
Have One Set of
Delegates.
rhe Question Greatly Affects
the Chances of Many
Candidates.
A. W. Bishop and Victor H. Metcalf
Have Opinions of Their
Own.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
90S Broadway, March 16.
Politicians are now Interested in the
methods in which the delegates to the
State and Congressional conventions
shall be elected, and whether one set of
delegates shall serve for both duties. On
this question opinion is divided, and upon
it depends to a marked degree the chances
of the county, gubernatorial and Con
ional candidates. The plan of "the
push" is to tie up tho county and guber
natorial candidates, have but one primary
for the county convention and name the
State and Congressional delegates at that
convention.
The Enquirer to-night says: "In the
lon of the friends of Dr. l'ardee
there is a gradual and certain drlu of
sentiment in his favor. The Pardee men j
are very confident that if the primary
wore to be held to-morrow Pardee would
; v. â¢ . p the county In a square contest be- ;
tween himself and Mr. Davis without in
t. rmixture of county politics In the
fight."
Dr. Pardee's friends are favoring the
county convention method of naming
State delegates, though their confidence
In their man is so great that it is hard to j
understand their eagerness for but one ;
convention. Victor H. Metcalf does not |
want any other issue but that of a Con
gressional nomination to enter Into his ,
right, and desires to have delegates
elected to the Congressional convention
with only himself and Mr. Hilboru as fac- i
tors.
Ex-Postmaster A. W. Bishop, who has
long been prominently identified with Re
publican politics, made the following
statement to-day:
There Is a difference of opinion in the j
piatter of the calling of conventions of the
Republican party of this county. Whether
it is best to call one or two conventions,
whether delegates shall be elected directly
to the State and Congres: i.niu.l conventions
ut the primaries, or whether the better way
would be. to call a county convention for
that purpose. To my mind the proper way
would le to call a convention. To go to the
State -ii.J Congressional conventions with a
li d delegation would be suicidal, as
lar as accomplishing anything for this
county is concerned. The Republicans of
this county have been unfortunate in the
j urt in this respect. With a divided dele
gation nothing can be accomplished. It
would be going there to be shorn. It Ala
â  County wants the Governorship, or
Congressman, or both, it will be necessary
that the delegation should go to the conven
tion solid, and the delegation should be
workers and fighters. With solidity and
as, success will attend their
tsâ otherwise they might as well re
. at home.
Republicans of Alameda County can
not agree among themselves, the balance of
t agree for them. We will
simply get scooped on every proposition.
My idea of the proper manner of conduct
thls nsrht Is, to call an early conven
or th^ election or' delegates to the
and Congressional conventionsâsepar
each. Then the candidates
".-is at the primaries will choose the
make their fight, and there will
- to who the candidate will
be, and the campaign will open at once,
with Alameda solid, in her choice for both
rj.or and â¢' "nKre?Fman, and no time
will be lost. < ttherwlse, a hard fight, un
certainty, with prospective defeat, awaits
us.
The county convention for selection of a
county ticket should be called tor a date no
â earlier than September. That convention
â k-ill have its hands full in selecting a
county ticket, without being mixed up with
the gubernatorial and Congressional fight.
This seems to me to be the proper course
of procedure, and one which will commend
itself to a very large majority of intelligent
Republicans of this county, and be a pro
moter of harmony and success in the com
ing battle at the polls. Any other course
will tangle up the political fight In an In
extricable snarl. __

A HERMIT
WITH RICHES
J_ived in Abject Penury
in a Lonely Hill
side Hut.
Taken to the Poorhouse, Where
He Died From Lack of
Nourishment.
An Estate of Two Thousand Dol
lars Is Now Found to Have
Been His.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, March 16.
A report was circulated yesterday that
James Selick. a hermit of Leona Heights, '
was missing from his cabin, where he has
lived alone for years. To-day it was
learned that the so-called pauper is dead
and left an estate of $2000, and Public Ad
ministrator Knight has applied for letters
of administration.
It was supposed that Selick had been
murdered, and people who had known
him searched the ravines, expecting to
find his body. It is now known that Selick
died at the County Infirmary on March
2, and was buried as a pauper.
Toward the end of February the man
was received at the hospital, -where he
had pone when he became sick, lie was
so ill that the Supervisors agreed to send
him to the County Infirmary. He was
dying for lack of nourishment and with
exhaustion, and he expired a few days
For several years he resided in a hut ;
near Mullen's ranch, about two miles i
south of the Kedwood road. He was ex- j
tremely penurious and miserly, and lived
in .squalor, denying himself proper food,
buying stale bread and but little meat.
He" occasionally worked at ouu jobs, but
after his death it was proved that there
was no reason for his miserliness. He has
told friends that he never married, and i
was about 72 years old, having been born |
on Prince Edwards Island.
Public Administrator Knight has ap- |
plied for letters on the old miser's estate, j
having discovered that there is an estate
consisting of mone>- and promissory notes |
worth about $2000. The expenses of burial
will be paid out of the estate, and an ef
fort will be made to find the heirs, if any
are living. |
A few months ago another inmate of
the County Infirmary died who was found
to have been possessed of $1200, part of
which was money in bank.
ACME MATCHES
CLEVER BOXERS
Lon Agnew and Ben Griffin
to Meet for Eight
Rounds.
Two Good Purses Offered â Savage
and De Coursey Also to
Fight
OAKLAND, March 16.â Friday evening !
the Acme Athletic Club will hold at its i
club rooms a grand carnival of boxrng.
The principal event will be an eight round
contest between Lon Agnew of Oakland <
and Ben Griffln of Oregon for a purse of I
$300;- a decision must be given. Both men j
have been in training for the past three
weeks, and are in condition to do the
battle of their lives. Lon Agnew's record
on this coast is well known, he having
defeated such men as Billy Akers, five
rounds; Fred Muller, six rounds; Bob j
Thompson, twelve rounds; Joe Kane, four j
rounds, and others. lie also fought a j
draw with Frank Purcell, of ten rounds,
and while in the East engaged in over j
fifty battles, winning them all. His only
defeats have been while suffering from a i
dislocated shoulder, rendering his right
arm almost useless. His last bout with
Joe Kane has shown that he is again in
proper form, and great results are ex
pected of him in his coming battles.
Ben Griffin is probably better known i
through the East and Northwest than he
Is in California. He has fought hundreds
of battles throughout those sections of |
the country, and is one of the cleverest
middleweig'hts of the present time. Some ,
of his most notable victories have been:
Harry Carr, three rounds; Charlie Mitch
ell two rounds: Tom Bernard, four
rounds; Bob Hughes, seven rounds; Harry
Harper, two rounds; Fred Clark, two
rounds; Pete Casey, three rounds; Jim |
Callahan, five rounds; Charlie Shutz, four
rounds; Billy Chambers, two rounds; :
Charlie Partlan, two rounds; Ed Hamlin, j
three rounds; Charlie Goff, three and two |
rounds. He also fought draws with
Tommy "West, six rounds; Will Evans,
eight rounds; Jimmy Handle, six rounds,
and Jack Hanley, six rounds.
There will also be a six round contest
for $200 between Yank Savage of Vallejo
and Billy De Coursey of Los Angeles.
Both of these men have been in training
for the past three weeks, and are In the
pink of condition. A decision must also
be rendered in this contest.
In the preliminaries there will be eight
three-round goes between well-known
boxers, and also some candidates for
pugilistic fame. The club has arranged
for extra seating capacity to accommo
date the large crowd that will be presr^
AXE SLASHING BATES AGAIN.
Insurance Compact Meets Opposition
in Alameda County.
OAKLAND, March 16.â The insurance
rate war has broken out afresh with all
of its old-time vigor. It will be the old
fight of the non-board companies against
the board companies, except that it has
for a leader one of the best-known board
companies' representatives. That the
board rates cannot stand in this city
when lower premiums can be had goes
without saying.
A. L. Stone of the Haywards electric
road is the leader in this movement. He
represents the Williamsburg City, the
Franklin and ihe Northwestern National
companies. These are outside of the
compact, and are at liberty to make any
rate they please.
This means a lively slashing of rates,
and no one can foretell the result. It is
even considered that if the fight is not
stopped in this county early it may ex
tend to San rranctsco and other cities,
breaking up the compact.
Swindled With Brass Spectacles.
OAKLAND, March 16. â The police
have been notified of a scheme of petty
swindling which has been practiced upon
the women of the homes principally.
Agents offering spectacles supposed to be
gold, but which are the rawest of brass,
will claim to have found a pair of gold
ones, which he will dispose of for almost
nothing. He usually secures $2 or $2 50.

range Entertaining and Inter
esting Topics.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call.
908 Broadway, .March 16.
The ladles of this city are very much
interested In the programme of the
Woman's Congress which will be held in
San Francisco during the first week of
May. The subject has already been
chosen and some sections of .. appor
tioned, but so far Oakland does not ap
pear to have been recognized, and it is
believed that the women of this city
should find some place on the programme.
The details of the congress will not be
made public for some days, and all the
State is waiting for them. There will,
however, be some keen disappointment
among those who expect to see much for
eign talent on the programme. The list
of speakers is confined almost exclu
sively to this State. Another cause of
disappointment in some quarters will be
the almost total elimination of the suf
frage question from the discussions. Only
in one paper that deals with suffrage and
its approach to American ideals Is the
subject broached, while the great topic
that extends through the whole three
days is "The Trend of American Social
Life."
Mrs. George "W. Burnell, the active
president of the Ebell Club, is an asso
ciate director of the State Woman's Con
gress, and she will undoubtedly see that
some of the talent on this side of the bay
is used. No known suffragist of promi
nence Is among the list of speakers, but
the congress will deal with the very
heart of American social life.
Dr. David S. Jordan is to take a promi
nent place, and with Mrs. George T.
Gaden, the secretary of the congress, is
now at work on the programme. The
most striking feature of the congress, as
at present arranged, is the apparent de
sire to keep suffrage in the background.
During the past three years there has
been much discussion as to whether the
suffrage question has not been overdone,
and judging from the present Indications
there is a desire not to repeat the mis
take. .

OAKLAND, March 16.â The Ebell So
ciety has arranged to give a grand recep

IT IS AN OLD "TIMER."
Historic Bell That Warns Travelers hu
the Ferrij Steamers,
IT has tnlled for the dead, rang many a merry peal at weddings, has
called peo-ple from far and near to praise and pray; its warning note has
been heard many a time and oft on the Sacramento River, and Stockton
people have often responded to its warning voice.
During foggy weather on the bay it guided vessels to a safe anchor
age from a point of vantage at the corner of Pacific and Davis, while for
years past the people of San Francisco and Oakland have alternately blessed
or cursed its clapper.
The best known bell in California and the one with the
strangest history is that which gives forth a warning peal
five minutes before the departure of each ferry steamer from
the foot of Market street and one stroke of the clapper as the
boat is leaving the ferry slip. Many a traveler laden with packages has exe
crated that stroke, while many and many a one has slacked his run to a se
date walk when the peal rang forth.
The bell was cast many years ago. It came to California to the order
of and bought by the First Congregational Church, which was then on the
corner of Dupont and California streets. It did duty there until the congre
gation moved to other and more congenial quarters. During the time it did
duty as a church bell it rang at the weddings of some of the best-known resi
dents of the city and tolled over the remains of some of our illustrious dead.
When the Academy of Sciences moved into the old church there was no
further use for the bell, and it hung rusting in its tower. About this time the
old river steamer Helen Hensley was built, and a bell for her could not be
purchased in any of the stores in the city. Some one thought of the church
bell, and the captain of the steamer was told he could have it for the trouble
of taking it away.
In its new sphere the bell continued to- do its duty, and year in and year
out it rang a warning note in foggy weather on the Sacramento and San
Joaquin rivers. When the Helen Hensley went into retirement in Oakland
Creek the bell was transferred to the Capital, and continued to do duty on
her until she had outlived her usefulness and was broken up.
Churches might change their abiding place and steamers grow old and
useless, but the bell was a goo-d bell yet, so it was removed from the Capital
and put in a tower at the corner of Davis and Pacific streets. At that time
the water of the bay came up to that point, and during foggy weather the, bell
was always rung to guide the steamers of the old California Steam Navigation
Company to- their wharves and vessels to their anchorage.
When the Southern Pacific bought out the Navigation Company it wanted
a bell for the ferry depot at the foot of Market street, so the church-steamer
fog bell was moved, and ever since it has rung for the departure of the Oak
land ferry boats. Very few of the inhabitants of California have never
heard it ring out, and many an old-timor who heard it ring in the First Con
gregational Church tower, again on thhe steamers Helen Hensley and Capital,
then as the fog bell at Davis and Pacific, and later in the ferry depot, says its
tones have mellowed with age, and that its prime of life has only just be
gun.
PREACHES FURITY TO THE NATION.
OAKLAND, March 16. â The three days' purity conference at the Y. M. C.
A. Hall, led by Mrs. M. E. Yeats, ended to-day. Mrs. Yeats is the national
evangelist of the department of purity of the W. C. T. U. In her addresses
she told of some remarkable experiences during fifteen years' experience in
prison and rescue work. She is one of the few speakers who can handle del
icate subjects without wounding the susceptibilities of the most refined. The
conference has been under the direction of the local W. C. T. U., and the
attendance at all the sessions has been unusually large. Mrs. Yeats is now
an a national tour.

KILLED BY THE LOCAL.
Fatal Result of Trying to Cross in
Front of a Moving Engine.
BERKELEY, March 16.â Mrs. Amelia
Clark died this afternoon at her residence
on Fifth Street in West Berkeley from the
Injuries which she received yesterday
morning by being struck by a local train
at Berkeley Station. Mrs. Clark was on
her way to her daily employment in San
Francisco and was approaching East
Berkeley Station as the 7 o'clock train
came down from North Berkeley. She
hastened to cross in front of the rapidly
approaching engine as it Is Impossible to
board the car.s from the west sides on
account of the gates which are always
kept closed on that side. She was struck
by the engine, receiving a fracture of the
skull and breaking three ribs. She was
taken into Baer's drugstore and later was
removed to her home, where Dr. J. S.
Eastman attended her.
As a result of the fatal accident to Mrs.
Clark the railroad company will probably
be petitioned to remove tne gates from
the local trains. Similar instances occur
daily and surprise has been expressed
that more such accidents have not oc
curred. Deputy Coroner Streightiff took
charge of the body this evening and will
hold an Inquest to-morrow.

BERKELEY, March 16.â Recorder Sut
ton of the university has completed an
examination of the records of the candi
dates for degrees at the coming com
mencement. According to his figures
there will be 231 students who will re
ceive degrees as against totals of 157 in
1597 and 129 in 189t>. Of the 231 degrees to
be conferred 189 are bachelor's degrees,
the others being masters and doctors.
Forty-seven per cent of those who en
tered in 1594 as regular freshmen of the
class of '98 will graduate. Besides these,
however, are a considerable number of
recruits from other classes and institu
tions and also students who entered with
the class of '9S as special or limited
students.

PIERCE IS EXAMINED-
Grand Jury Asks the Contra Costa
Man About Alleged Bribes.
OAKLAND, March 16.â The Grand Jury
adjourned this afternoon till next Tues
day. This morning Henry Pierce, presi
dent of the Contra Costa Water Com
pany, was a witness. He arrived before
Councilman Henneberry had been ex
cused, and showed some impatience, but
was told he would have to wait his turn.
Mr. Pierce was before the jury for
nearly one hour. He said he told the
Jurors that his company had not been
paying 3 per cent on the capital invested
for several years. He ventured the sug
gestion that a man should be permitted
to sell water without restriction as to
price, as another man would sell meat.
Councilman Earle was the next witness.
Hcitman and "Watkinson were also in at
tendance, but were not questioned.

A KLONDIKE AT THEIB FEET.
Residents in the Vicinity of Mount
Olympus Excited Over an Al
leged Gold Discovery.
â Why j?o to the Klondike and suffer Its
many dangers when mines are being dis
covered nearly every day in our ' own
Golden State? Is a problem which should
make future travelers to that region stop
and consider.
The latest find of what is believed to be
an outcropping of the mother lode was
made by Contractors Ferris and Williams
while blasting at the base of Mt. Olym
pus in this city a few days ago. As soon
as the discovery of the new gold field be
came known there was an outpouring of
the residents of Ashbury and Clarendon
Heights, re-enforced by prospective min
ors from all parts of the city. People who
could not tell sulphurets from the yellow
metal visited the scene yesterday armed
with prospecting tools of every conceiv
able nature and a fund of Information
which they were willing to impart gratis
to willing ears. One enthusiast after ex
amining a specimen wildly stated that the
ore would run from $150 to $200 per ton.
When the contractors heard this they
immediately went on a quiet hunt for the
expert in the hope of disposing of a few
tons of the street-filling rock to him at a
less figure than his value, but he had es
caped.
A few samples of the rock were taken
to a local assayer for an opinion as to its
value, and his decision is awaited anx
iously by the contractors and the peop'e
living in* the vicinity of the find, who are
indulging in roseate dreams of the untold
wealth lying at their feet.
A caterpillar is so greedy that in one
month it usually devours six thousand
times its own weight in food.
SMALL TROUBLE
BROUGHT DEATH
Suicide of a Young Man Be
cause of Trivial Business
Reverses.
â¢erdinand Mayer Leaves His Bride
of Three Weeks a Sorrowing
Widow Without Warning.
Because of business reverses Ferdinand
Mayer cut himself off yesterday in the
prime of his youth, only twenty-six sum
mers having passed over his head. Three
weeks ago Mayer stood at the altar with
his beautiful bride of nineteen summers
and promised to love, cherish and protect
her. Yesterday his bleeding body, with a
bullet hole in the right temple, lay on a
slab in the Morgue and the word "sui
cide" was inscribed against his name on
the record.
Mayer had been a partner of Philip
Deller for about two years and trans
acted a restaurant and butcher business
under the firm name of Mayer & Deller,
at 131 and 135 Fifth street. About ten
days ago the partners agreed to dissolve,
the business not having been profitable.
Deller took the restaurant at 135 Fifth
street and Mayer took the butcher shop
at 131 Fifth street, over which he resided.
The dissolution seemed to pray on
Mayer's mind, but it was not thought by
his wife or his friends that he contem
plated doing away with himself. Last
Tuesday at his request his wife borrowed
$500 from her uncle for the purpose of
paying off his share of the debts of the
firm. Two hundred dollars of this sum
were paid out on that day and it was
Mayer's intention to pay the remaining
$300 yesterday. On Tuesday night the
couple retired at about 10 o'clock in good
spirits.
Early yesterday morning Mrs. Mayer
arose for the purpose of admitting John
Bardin, Mayer's assistant, to the butchef
shop. Mayer called down stairs to him
to order a half-gallon of milk from tha
milkman when he came. Mrs. Mayer was
busying herself downstairs about her
household duties. When breakfast was
ready she called her husband, but there
was no response, and she went upstairs.
She was surprised to find that he was not
in the bedroom, but on looking Into tha
clothes closet she saw him lying dead,
huddled up in a corner with the blood
trickling sluggishly from a bullet hole in
the right temple. A revolver in the right
hand of the dead man showed how the
wound had been produced.
Mrs. Mayer was almost insane with the
shock of the terrible affliction which fell
upon her without the slightest warning
and she was taken In charge by her rela
tives.
Coroner Hawkins will hold an Inquest
on Saturday morning.

RECREATION
IN FORM AGAIN
The San Pablo Stake an
Exercise Gallop for the
Fast Filly.
A'varado Furnished a Surprise
by Winning at Odds of
13 to 1.
Three Favorites Reached the Wire
in Frontâ Bonibel Scored at
Long Odds.
The sweet fllly Recreation ma.de a play
ground of the San Pablo selling stake,
over seven hurdles, decided at Oakland
yesterday. The daughter of Morello was
taken Fast last year by Burns & Water
house with a view of landing the rich
Futurity stake, but a few days before
the big event was to be decided she went
amiss and was shelved for a long time.
She has apparently rounded to, and from
now on will be hard to beat. In yester
day's stake the crack filly was entered to
be sold for $1000, which, according to the
conditions, allowed her in with 105 pounds
up- Highland Ball and Lord Marmion
were the only other starters, and 1 to 5
was the post price against her. Clayton,
on Lord Marmion, went out to make the
favorite slop, but after she had carried
him to the half In :47U and the five fur
longs in 1:00)4 Marmion gave it up. Tne
first choice then won in hollow fashion
by three lengths, in 1:28*4-
The weather lvas cool, but the horses
unlimbered in their preliminary gallops,
and the majority of the races were run in
fast time. The afternoon's proceedings
were enlivened by the clever victories
of Alvarado II and Bonibel at long odds,
while favorites managed to hold their
own by capturing three purses.
Nine started in the opening six-furlong
sprint, and Midian, starting for the first
time in the colors of Tom Ryan, carried
the wise money, being backed down from
7 to 5 to even money. Conley got the
chestnut colt away well, and maintaining
his lead throughout, won easily from Chi
huahua, which, with Baracan, shared the
honors of holding second place in the
betting.
The Burns & Waterhouse entry, Palo
macita, was In strong demand in the ring
during the speculating on the mile and a
furlong run for mares, the big coin send
ing her to the post a 4 to 5 choice.
Treachery, the second choice, with Tod
Sloan in ..ue saddle, had the footing over
her Reid from the start, and finally
downed the favorite with ease. "Skeets"
Martin took the show with Coda.
EL L. Frank, the Montana turfman.
went up and down the line on his colt, B.
Come, the even money favorite for the
two-year-old event at four furlongs. Cash
Sloan pot him pocketed at the start, and
he never proved a factor. Buena Ven
tura led to within fifty yards <>f the wire,
where Bonibel, the Burns <Jc Waterhouse
entry, got up and won by a couple of
lengths. The winner went to the post at
odds of 20 to 1.
The mile and a sixteenth' selling run
proved a proi-^c source of income to the
books, for it went to the 15 to 1 outsider,
Alvarado 11. with the heavily played ones
bringing up behind him. Parthamax was
sent out a 9 to 1 favorite, but displayed
little speed, and ran unplaced. Mamie
Bcott showed first into the stretch, where
phe was overhauled and led past tne
stand a length by tne Sink entry. Key
del Tierra got away with the show hon
ors.
The last race of tne day, a seven-fur
long selling event, fell to McT.ipht. the
even mom*y choice. The chestnut horse
made most of the running, and at the
wire won, hard pressed, half a length in
front of Dr. MarKs. Refugee was a close
third.
TRACK NOTES.
The cases of the suspended riders. Spencer
and Jones, will not come before the Board
of Stewards until next week some time, so it
v.as given out from the stand yesterday.
The bookmakins? ranks are thinning out. But
nineteen firms cut In yesterday. George Rose
now has but one book in the big ring.
Judge Joe Murphy thinks the Burns &
Waterhouse pair. Traversf-r and Recreation,
will be hard to beat in the California Derby,
to be decided in the near future at Ingleslde.

THE SAN FRAXCISCO OAIX, THURSDAY, MARCH IT, 1898.
GRIPMEN DODGE
SNOWY SPHERES
Snowstorm and Hilarity
in the Western
Addition.
Frosts Do Unexpected Damage
in Various Sections of
the State.
Peculiar Phases of the Weather Make
Forecasting Difficultâ Deep
Fall at Summit.
A white mantle of sleet came down
from the clouds yesterday and fooled
the weather prophet along with the
rest. Not all sections of the city re
ceived the same treatment, for only a
few light 'flakes of snow fell in the
downtown region, and these melted as
soon as they touched the pavement, but
in the Western Addition, Presidio
Heights and Russian Hill districts the
fall was heavy enough to remain on
the ground for some time.
At about 10 a. m. a mixture of snow
and hail began to fall in that part of
the city lying generally between Hyde
street and Central avenue, and kept
coming down until in some places the
depth was two inches. All the roofs
of that part of the city soon assumed
the appearance of having been white
washed, and the small boy was
in his element. On the Jack
son and Clay street lines the grip
men had all they could do to dodge
snowballs, hurled by hilarious urchins.
Even as far eastward as the Powell
street line, in the North Beach region,
the gripmen came in for an occasional
missile. The first intimation the peo
ple in the marts of business had of the
fact that the western part of the city
was experiencing a snowfall was
when Western Addition cars came in
with roofs and platforms covered with
the white material. Then the news
boys crowded round, scooped up great
handfuls of snow and made things
warm for pedestrians, especially those
laboring under the burden of silk hats.
Out in the snow district even the wom
en took a hand in affairs, and those
who jumped aboard cars and went out
there to have a little fun found that
being bespattered with the mud and
slush of a tenth-rate snowstorm was
not so much fun after all.
The weather phenomena observed all
over the State were fully as peculiar
as the local variations. At Eureka and
Red Bluff such a peculiar contradiction
in the weather was observed as heavy
showers during the night and killing;
frosts early in the morning. Scattered
showers fell north of Tehachapi and
around San Diego, though nowhere else
in the southern part of the State.
In Napa and Sonoma counties' there
were severe frosts. Sacramento came
in for a light touch, and along the
coast from Gilroy south to San Luis
Obispo the weather was freezing. It
was also pretty cold in a few spots in
the San Joaquin Valley.
It snowed heavily in the mountains
north and east, and at 7 a. m. the snow
at Summit was fifty inches deep and
six inches in depth in Blue Canyon.
Forecast Official Hammon, who fur
nished the data given in the preced
ing, says the damage done by the frost
throughout the State is not likely to
be great on account of the changeable
ness of the weather. Apricots, al
monds and some varieties of peaches
will suffer the most.
The fall of sleet on Presidio Heights
is easy of explanation. Mr. Hammon
states that a current of cold air passed
between the nimbus cloud and the
earth, and the rain became frozen as It
passed through it. The hail stones were
very large, and are reported to have
broken in pieces as they hit the hard
pavements.
TERRILL WILL
RIDE MARTIN
The California Cycler Accepts
the Australian's Chal
lenge.
Martin Also Wants to RldÂ« Against
the Running Horse Prince
Hooker.
The challenge of William Martin, the
celebrated cyclist who arrived here last
week from Australia, and which was pub
lished exclusively in The Call Saturday,
seems likely to be productive of a match
race of more than ordinary interest, and
the wheelmen hereabouts may have the
opportunity of seeing this remarkable
"plugger" go against one of our crack
local riders, "Bob" Terrill.
Terrill will accept Martin's offer to race
from one to five miles, best two in three
heats, for a side bet and a purse, and will
ride him In two weeks. The only draw
back to the match lies in the fact that
both are L. A. W. men and cannot race
on Sunday under its rules. The "gate"
at a Saturday afternoon meet might not
pay a promoter to hang up a very attrac
tive purse. But Martin says if the side
bet is large enough he doesn't care a rap
about the purse. The Elmhurst track
people are now dealing with the principals
and hope to bring off the match across
the bay.
As soon as Martin leaves here he is
going south to race the horse Prince
Hooker, whose owner offers all expenses
and $1000 to any cycler who with pacing
can beat the horse three ont of five heats
at from one to five miles. He is a run
ning horse, attached to a sulky, and a
wheelman has got to beat 1:50 for a mile
to defeat him. All the cyclers in the
south, mounted on everything from a
single wheel to a sextet, have been up
against this horse and been beaten. Mar
tin thinks he can do the trick handilly,
however, if he can get pacemakers, and,
as usual, is willing to bet a little of his
own money on the side.
Advances made on furniture and pianos, with
or without removal. J. Nnnnan. 1017-1023 Mission.
Visiting the President.
Mrs. George E. Morse and daughter,
Marjorie McKlnley Morse, arrived in
Washington last night, where they will
spend some time in visiting the President
and his family. Mrs. Morse is a niece of
the President.

PERTINENT QUESTIONS AND INTERESTING ANSWERS.
A SEARCH FOR MORE LIGHT.
The construction of the ferry building Is supposed to be
supervised by a first-class architect, who receives a liberal
compensation for his services. Is It possible that he has per
mitted the Irregularities daily dwelt upon by a contemporary
to pass without remonstrance? Has he consented to changes

State should have the benefit of a great reduction if the com
moner lumber were used instead of the better and more costly?
These are all pertinent inquiries and should be answered.
There has unquestionably been a lot of "sculduggery," and
the public has a right to know whether the man whose busi
ness it was to prevent it was remiss In his duties. â From
